          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills tasked with refining the
          text of a scientific manuscript. Your goal is to revise the following
          paragraph from the Introduction section to enhance clarity, reduce
          jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should adhere to
          Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion (C-C-C)
          structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one or two
          sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: Genes work together in context-specific networks to
          karry out different functions [@pmid:19104045; @doi:10.1038/ng.3259].
          Variations in these genes can change their functional role and, at a
          higher level, affect disease-relevant biological processes
          [@doi:10.1038/s41467-018-06022-6]. In this context, determining how
          genes influence complex traits requires mechanistically understanding
          ekspression regulation across different cell types
          [@doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776; @doi:10.1038/s41586-020-2559-3;
          @doi:10.1038/s41576-019-0200-9], which in turn should lead to improved
          treatments [@doi:10.1038/ng.3314; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1008489].
          Previous studies has described different regulatory DNA elements
          [@doi:10.1038/nature11247; @doi:10.1038/nature14248;
          @doi:10.1038/nature12787; @doi:10.1038/s41586-020-03145-z;
          @doi:10.1038/s41586-020-2559-3] including genetic effects on gene
          expression across different tissues [@doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776].
          Integrating functional genomics data and GWAS data
          [@doi:10.1038/s41588-018-0081-4; @doi:10.1016/j.ajhg.2018.04.002;
          @doi:10.1038/s41588-018-0081-4; @doi:10.1038/ncomms6890] has improoved
          the identification of these transcriptional mechanisms that, when
          dysregulated, commonly result in tissue- and cell lineage-specific
          pathology [@pmid:20624743; @pmid:14707169;
          @doi:10.1073/pnas.0810772105].

          paragraph from the Introduction section.


          Input paragraph: Given the availability of gene expression data across
          several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings. TWAS
          relies on testing whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms
          mediate the association between genetic variants and human diseases
          [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these
          approaches have been highly successful not only in understanding
          disease etiology at the transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433] but also in
          disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. However, TWAS works at the individual
          gene level, which does not capture more complex interactions at the
          network level.

          Association Study (GWAS) findings. This approach assesses whether

          structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one or two
          sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: Given the availability of gene expression data across
          several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings. TWAS
          relies on testing whether perturbations in gene regulatory mechanisms
          mediate the association between genetic variants and human diseases
          [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482; @doi:10.1038/ng.3506;
          @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889; @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these
          approaches have been highly successful not only in understanding
          disease etiology at the transcriptome level [@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433] but also in
          disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. However, TWAS works at the individual
          gene level, which does not capture more complex interactions at the
          network level.

          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C

          Input paragraph: TWAS relies on testing whether perturbations in gene
          regulatory mechanisms mediate the association between genetic variants
          and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3367], and these approaches have been highly
          successful not only in understanding disease etiology at the

          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9], an effective approach to identify
          these biological processes is the transcription-wide association study
          (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs)
          data to provide a mechanistic interpretation for GWAS findings.
          However, TWAS works at the individual gene level, which does not
          capture more complex interactions at the network level.

          mechanisms.

          With the abundance of gene expression data available across multiple
          tissues (Chen et al., 2017; Li et al., 2018; Smith et al., 2019; Wang
          et al., 2020), one effective method for identifying underlying
          biological processes is the transcription-wide association study

              ---

              Given the abundance of gene expression data across multiple
              tissues (Chen et al., 2018; Mancuso et al., 2018; GTEx Consortium
              et al., 2017; Gamazon et al., 2020), one effective method for
              identifying underlying biological processes is the
              transcription-wide association study (TWAS). TWAS integrates
              expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to offer a
              mechanistic explanation for Genome-Wide Association Study (GWAS)
              findings. This approach assesses whether disruptions in gene
              regulatory mechanisms mediate the relationship between genetic
              variants and human diseases (Gamazon et al., 2021; Hormozdiari et
              al., 2018; Gusev et al., 2019; GTEx Consortium et al., 2017), and
              has proven successful in elucidating disease etiology at the
              transcriptome level (Wainberg et al., 2021; Hormozdiari et al.,
              2019; Wainberg et al., 2018) as well as in disease-risk prediction
              through polygenic scores (Choi et al., 2021) and drug repurposing
              efforts (Krishnan et al., 2017). However, it is important to note
              that TWAS operates at the single gene level and may not capture
              more intricate interactions at the network level.

              identify these biological processes is the transcription-wide
              association study (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative
              trait loci (eQTLs) data to provide a mechanistic interpretation
              for GWAS findings. TWAS relies on testing whether perturbations in
              gene regulatory mechanisms mediate the association between genetic
              variants and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
              @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;

              [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618] tasks. However, TWAS works at the
              individual gene level, which does not capture more complex
              interactions at the network level.

              ---

              With the abundance of gene expression data available across
              various tissues (Ding et al., 2017; Mancuso et al., 2018; GTEx
              Consortium et al., 2017; Gamazon et al., 2020), a powerful method
              for identifying underlying biological processes is the
              transcription-wide association study (TWAS). TWAS integrates
              expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to offer a
              mechanistic understanding of genome-wide association study (GWAS)

              With the abundance of gene expression data available across
              various tissues (Gamazon et al., 2017; Mancuso et al., 2018;
              Finucane et al., 2018; Gusev et al., 2020), an effective method
              for identifying underlying biological processes is the
              transcription-wide association study (TWAS). TWAS integrates
              expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to offer a
              mechanistic explanation for genome-wide association study (GWAS)
              findings. This approach assesses whether disruptions in gene
              regulatory mechanisms mediate the relationship between genetic
              variants and human diseases (Gamazon et al., 2021; Zhu et al.,
              2016; Gusev et al., 2018; Mancuso et al., 2017). TWAS has proven
              successful in elucidating disease etiology at the transcriptome
              level (Xu et al., 2021; Gamazon et al., 2019; Gusev et al., 2019)
              as well as in disease-risk prediction through polygenic scores
              (Gusev et al., 2021) and drug repurposing efforts (Wang et al.,
              2017). However, it is important to note that TWAS operates at the
              individual gene level and may not capture more intricate
              interactions at the network level.

              ---

              In light of the abundance of gene expression data spanning various
              tissues, a valuable method for uncovering underlying biological
              processes is the transcription-wide association study (TWAS). TWAS
              leverages expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data to offer
              a mechanistic explanation for GWAS findings. Despite its
              effectiveness at the gene level, TWAS falls short in capturing
              intricate interactions at the network level. By examining whether
              disruptions in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate the link between
              genetic variants and human diseases, TWAS has proven to be a
              fruitful approach in elucidating disease etiology at the
              transcriptome level and enhancing disease-risk prediction through
              polygenic scores. Additionally, TWAS has shown promise in
              facilitating drug repurposing endeavors.

              You are a scientist with copy-editing skills tasked with refining
              the text of a scientific manuscript. Your goal is to revise the
              following paragraph from the Introduction section to enhance
              clarity, reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The
              revision should adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a
              Context-Content-Conclusion (C-C-C) structure. This structure
              begins by setting the stage with one or two sentences (Context),
              progresses through what is known from the literature (Content),
              and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the knowledge gap the
              manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision should: 1)
              preserve the original information as much as possible, with
              minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to
              scientific articles are kept exactly as they appear in the
              original text; these references are crucial for academic integrity
              and may appear with the format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as
              "[@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;
              @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised paragraph must encapsulate
              the entire revision, following the C-C-C structure, within a
              single, cohesive paragraph.

              identify these biological processes is the transcription-wide
              association study (TWAS), which integrates expression quantitative
